Letter Substitution Without Meaning Change in Prayer
Ruling on letter substitution without meaning change in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority ruling on substituting letters in Quranic recitation without changing meaning and explain the minority opinion of Abu Sa'id.

Core Idea
Substituting a letter without changing meaning does not invalidate the prayer.

When reciting the Quran in prayer, precision in letters is generally required. However, what happens if a worshipper inadvertently substitutes one letter for another that does not alter the meaning? This lesson examines the Hanafi majority position that the prayer remains valid, and a minority view that considers it invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what the act is (substituting a letter in Quranic recitation during prayer) and the majority ruling (the prayer is not invalidated).
The example given is saying 'iyab' instead of 'awwab'.
2
Understand the basis for the ruling
The key condition is that the meaning must not change. The majority consider the meaning preserved, so the prayer remains valid.
'Iyab' and 'awwab' both mean 'returning'.
3
Note the minority disagreement
Abu Sa'id held that even such a substitution invalidates the prayer. Understand the basis of his stricter view.
His reasoning is not given in the passage but likely based on strict adherence to exact letter sequence.
4
Determine the praktical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: the majority view does not require repetition or compensation; the minority view requires repeating the prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢٦] وان كان بوضع حرف مكان حرف ولم يتغير المعنى نحو, اياب مكان, اوّاب لاتفسد .. وعن ابي سعيد تفسد ...
[26] And if it was by placing a letter in place of a letter and the meaning did not change, like 'iyab' in place of 'awwab', it does not invalidate. And from Abu Sa'id: it invalidates.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and a minority disagreement regarding substituting one letter for another in Quranic recitation during prayer without altering the meaning. The example given is reading 'iyab' (return) instead of 'awwab' (oft-returning) in Surah Sad verse 19 (38:19). Since the meaning does not change—both imply returning—the majority view is that the prayer is not invalidated. However, Abu Sa'id (probably Abu Sa'id al-Bardha'i or similar) held the stricter view that it does invalidate. The relied-upon position is that it does not invalidate, as the meaning remains intact.

Key Terms
حرف (harf): A letter of the Arabic alphabet.
معنى (ma'na): The meaning or sense of the word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid.
B) The prayer is valid and no compensation is needed.
C) The prayer requires sajdah al-sahw.
D) The prayer is valid only if done unintentionally.

Key Takeaway
When reciting Quran in prayer, substituting a letter with another that does not alter the meaning does not invalidate the prayer according to the majority Hanafi view.

Common Recitation Mistakes Among Laypeople
Ruling on common recitation mistakes by villagers, Turks, and Sudanese

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on common recitation errors made by laypeople and explain the basis of leniency (raf' al-haraj).

Core Idea
Common recitation errors in Salah do not invalidate the prayer — a fatwa of ease for laypeople.

You may have heard that certain mistakes in Quranic recitation can break your prayer. But what about the inevitable errors made by non-Arab Muslims who struggle with Arabic pronunciation? In this passage, later Hanafi scholars address exactly this concern, ruling that such common mistakes do not invalidate the prayer, even if the meaning changes slightly. This reflects the school's principle of removing hardship from the community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the errors described
Note the specific mistakes: pronouncing 'iyyaka na'bud' as 'wiyyaka na'bud' (waw instead of hamza) and adding 'al- to 'sirat alladhina' (making it 'al-sirat alladhina').
2
State the ruling
The scholars explicitly state that these errors do not invalidate the prayer (la fasad).
The ruling applies even though the meaning may change.
3
Identify the rationale
The ruling is based on the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj) for common people who find it difficult to correct habitual pronunciation.
This is a fatwa of later Hanafi scholars, not a textually-derived ruling from the early authorities.
4
Consider the scope of leniency
The leniency covers common, habitual errors. It does not apply to deliberate changes or corrections that are easily avoidable.
The passage focuses on errors that are widespread among certain groups (villagers, Turks, Sudanese).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷] وكثيراًما يقع فى قرأة بعض القرويين و الاتراک و السودان و یاک نعبد، بواو مكان الهمزة ، و الصراط الذين ، بزيادة الالف و اللام ، و صرحوا في الصورتين بعدم الفساد و ان غير المعنى .
[27] And it frequently occurs in the recitation of some villagers, Turks, and Sudanese: 'wiyyaka na'bud' with a waw in place of the hamza, and 'al-sirat alladhina' with an addition of alif and lam, and they explicitly stated in both cases that there is no invalidation even if the meaning changes.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage addresses common recitation mistakes observed among non-Arab Muslims, specifically villagers, Turks, and Sudanese, who often pronounce 'iyyaka na'bud' (You alone we worship) as 'wiyyaka na'bud' (with waw instead of hamza) and add the definite article 'al-' to 'sirat alladhina' (the path of those) making it 'al-sirat alladhina'. The scholars explicitly ruled that these errors do not invalidate the prayer, even though the meaning may be slightly altered. This is based on the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj) for laypeople who may not be able to correct their pronunciation. The commentary adds that once a wrong pronunciation becomes habitual, it is difficult to change, so the lenient ruling preserves the validity of their prayers.

Key Terms
قرويون (qaraweeyun): Villagers or rural people, likely from the Indian subcontinent.
زيادة (ziyadah): Addition of letters (here, alif and lam) to the Quranic text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid despite the error.
C) The prayer is invalid only if done knowingly.
D) The prayer requires sajdah al-sahw.

Key Takeaway
Common recitation errors that are habitual for laypeople do not invalidate salah, reflecting the Hanafi principle of removing hardship.

Three Acts That Do Not Invalidate Prayer
Listing of three actions that do not break prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three specific actions that do not invalidate prayer according to the Hanafi school and identify the condition that limits each exception.

Core Idea
Looking at writing, swallowing small food remnants, and someone passing in front do not break prayer provided conditions are met.

In this passage, we learn that while many actions can nullify prayer, three exceptions are explicitly given. These exceptions hinge on the absence of excessive movement, the negligible quantity of the act, or the passive nature of the event. Understanding these boundaries preserves the integrity of salah while accommodating minor human needs and distractions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions
Read the passage and list the three actions described as not invalidating prayer: (1) looking at a written text and understanding its meaning, (2) eating food remnants between teeth that are less than a chickpea in size, (3) someone passing in front of the praying person.
The third action is implied by 'or passes (مرّ)' which refers to a person crossing in front.
2
Determine the condition for each action
For the first action, there is no condition explicitly stated except that understanding is allowed; it must not lead to excessive movement or recitation. For the second, the condition is the amount must be less than a chickpea and done without much action (amal kathir). For the third, the action is from the passer, not the person in prayer.
The condition of 'amal kathir' applies to the second action explicitly; for the first, it is implicit that looking itself is not excessive movement.
3
Understand the ruling
The overall ruling is that these actions do not invalidate the prayer (sahih). The prayer remains valid, and no compensation (like sajdah al-sahw) is required.
This is based on Hanafi principles that minor actions (amal qalil) are excused.
4
Connect to broader fiqh
These exceptions fit within the Hanafi framework that differentiates between amal kathir (invalidates) and amal qalil (does not). The chickpea measurement (himmisa) is a standard benchmark for negligible quantities.
This passage is a memorization anchor for students to remember key exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: لا يفسد الصلوة تمامه فيه فليراجع ، و الله سبحانه تعالى اعلم و استغفر الله العظيم . فصل : (۵۵۰) لو نظر المصلى الى مكتوب وفهمه او اكل ما بين اسنانه دون الحمّصة بلا عمل كثير او مرّ
Section: The prayer is not invalidated... its completion is in it, so refer to it. And Allah the Glorious and Exalted knows best, and I seek forgiveness of Allah the Mighty. Section: (550) If the praying person looks at a written text and understands it, or eats what is between his teeth less than a chickpea without much action, or passes [a person before him]...
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage begins a new section (fasl) listing three actions that do not invalidate the prayer, based on Hanafi fiqh. The first is looking at written material and understanding its meaning without reciting it aloud; this is permissible as long as it does not involve excessive physical movement or vocalization. The second is consuming food remnants caught between teeth that are less than the size of a chickpea, as it is considered akin to swallowing saliva. The third is a person passing in front of the praying individual; while the passer commits a sin, the prayer of the person praying remains valid. The commentary adds that the first ruling is based on the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) gestured to Abu Bakr during prayer, and Abu Bakr understood and continued, proving that understanding writing does not invalidate.

Key Terms
عمل كثير (amal kathir): Excessive physical movement beyond the usual actions of prayer.
حمصة (himmisa): A chickpea; used as a measurement for small items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens if a person passes directly in front of someone in prayer?
A) The person praying's prayer becomes invalid.
B) The passer commits a sin, but the prayer remains valid.
C) Both the prayer and the passer's action are invalid.
D) The prayer requires sajdah al-sahw.

Key Takeaway
Three minor actions—looking at writing, swallowing small residues, and someone passing—do not invalidate prayer, provided the conditions of size and movement are met.

Mispronunciation of Fatiha by Rural People Does Not Invalidate Prayer
Ruling that prayer is valid despite dialectal mispronunciation in Fatiha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer validity when mispronunciation is due to dialect and apply the principle of istihsan based on hardship.

Core Idea
Dialectal mispronunciation in Fatiha does not invalidate prayer because hardship is removed by istihsan.

Many Muslims around the world recite the Fatiha with regional accents or dialectal variations. This passage from a classical Hanafi commentary addresses a common concern: does a habitual mispronunciation that changes the meaning invalidate the prayer? The Hanafi school provides a lenient ruling, rooted in the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj) and istihsan (juristic preference). Understanding this ruling helps us appreciate the flexibility of Islamic law in accommodating natural speech patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the recitation of Surah al-Fatiha in prayer. The passage states that even when the pronunciation changes the meaning (e.g., 'wayyaka' instead of 'iyyaka'), the prayer is not invalidated (fasid).
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The evidence for this ruling is istihsan, based on the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj). The Quran says 'Allah intends ease for you, not hardship' (2:185).
The dalil is not a specific text but a general legal principle.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the principle apply? Invalidating the prayer would cause extreme hardship for rural people who cannot correct their ingrained pronunciation. The law prioritizes preventing hardship over strict adherence to correct recitation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require correct pronunciation and consider such mispronunciation invalid if it changes the meaning. The Hanafi school differs due to the consideration of dialect and habit.
The cross-madhhab difference is noted in the hanafi_position_box.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is that the prayer is valid (sahih) and not to be repeated. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) for fatwa, especially in regions with dialectal variations.
The authority weight is fatawa_mutakhkhirin (later fatwa literature).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح : جھارکھنڈ کے دیہاتی اور ترکی اور سوڈانی لوگ ایاک نعبد میں ہمزہ کے بجائے ویاک نعبد واو کے ساتھ پڑھتے ہیں تو اس سے چاہے معنی بدل جائے پھر بھی نماز فاسد نہیں ہوگی، کیونکہ فاسد کرنے سے دیہاتیوں کو حرج میں مبتلا کرنا لازم ہوگا، اسی طرح و صراط الذین“ میں الف لام نہیں ہے، لیکن دیہاتی لوگ والصراط الذین الف لام کے ساتھ پڑھتے ہیں تو اس سے نماز فاسد نہیں ہوگی، تا کہ یہ بیچارے دیہاتی کی نماز زندگی بھر درست رہے۔ اور ایک عجیب بات ہے کہ کوئی غلط لفظ ایک مرتبہ منہ پر چڑھ جانے کے بعد بہت کوشش کے باوجود بھی درست نہیں کر پاتا۔
Explanation: Rural people of Jharkhand and Turkish and Sudanese people recite 'iyyaka na'budu' with a waw instead of hamza as 'wayyaka na'budu'. Even if the meaning changes, the prayer is not invalidated because invalidating it would necessitate putting the rural people in difficulty. Similarly, in 'wa sirata alladhina' there is no alif-lam, but rural people recite 'wa al-sirata alladhina' with alif-lam, yet the prayer is not invalidated, so that the poor rural person's prayer remains valid throughout life. And it is a strange thing that once a wrong word becomes fixed on the tongue, despite much effort, it cannot be corrected.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (tashrih) on a ruling about mispronunciation in prayer. The author states that certain rural populations habitually pronounce 'iyyaka na'budu' as 'wayyaka na'budu' (with a waw instead of hamza), and 'al-sirata' as 'al-sirata' with the definite article even where the Quran has none. Even though the meaning changes, the prayer remains valid. The ruling is based on the Hanafi principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj) and istihsan (juristic preference). Forcing these people to correct their pronunciation would cause undue difficulty, and since their recitation is habitual and not intentional, the prayer is valid. The author adds a note that such mispronunciations are often impossible to correct once ingrained. This reflects the Hanafi approach of accommodating local dialects and avoiding excessive rigidity.

Key Terms
حرج (haraj): Hardship or difficulty that would arise if the prayer were invalidated due to dialectal mispronunciation.
فاسد (fasid): Invalid (of prayer); the passage rules that the prayer is not invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A rural person habitually pronounces 'iyyaka na'budu' as 'wayyaka na'budu' in prayer. According to the Hanafi ruling in the passage, what is the status of his prayer?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid because invalidating would cause hardship.
C) The prayer is valid only if he corrects it after being reminded.
D) The prayer is invalid if the meaning changes.

Key Takeaway
Prayer remains valid when mispronunciation is due to ingrained dialect, to avoid hardship.

Three Acts That Do Not Invalidate Prayer
Explanation of three actions that do not invalidate prayer with their proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three actions that do not invalidate prayer according to the Hanafi school and identify the proof for each.

Core Idea
Small actions like looking, eating a tiny amount, or someone passing do not break prayer.

In this passage, we learn about three specific situations that commonly raise doubts about the validity of salah. The Hanafi school clarifies that these small actions—looking at writing, eating a negligible amount, or having someone pass in front—do not invalidate the prayer, provided they do not involve excessive movement. We will examine the rulings and the evidence from the Sunnah that supports them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions
List the three actions the author states do not invalidate prayer: looking at writing, eating food stuck between teeth less than a chickpea, and someone passing in front.
2
Understand the reasoning for each
For each action, note why it is considered a small action (amal qalil) and how it differs from excessive action (amal kathir).
The hadith of the Prophet carrying Umama proves that small movements do not invalidate.
3
Review the proofs (adillah)
The author cites several hadith: the gesture of the Prophet to Abu Bakr, the carrying of Umama, and hadith about passing not cutting off prayer.
The hadith of Abu Bakr is used as an analogy for understanding written text.
4
Note the exceptions
If the action becomes excessive (e.g., eating more than a chickpea, multiple steps), it invalidates prayer.
The threshold for eating is the size of a chickpea.
5
Apply the ruling
Consider real-life scenarios: a worshipper looks at a phone screen, swallows a tiny piece of food, or someone crosses in front. Determine validity.
Remember that the passer commits a sin, but the prayer remains valid.

Section: (550) If the one praying looks at a written text and understands it, or eats what is between his teeth without a chickpea (i.e., less than a chickpea) without excessive action, or someone passes in the place of prostration... Explanation: Here there are three issues, and the ruling for all three is that the prayer is not invalidated. [1] The one praying looked at a written sentence and understood it but did not recite it aloud; the prayer is not invalidated because there was no teaching or learning, nor excessive action. [2] There was meat etc. between his teeth less than a chickpea; eating it does not invalidate the prayer because it is like saliva. [3] Someone passed in the place of prostration; the prayer is not invalidated, but the passer commits a sin because there is no excessive action. Proofs: (1) The Prophet (peace be upon him) gestured to Abu Bakr during prayer and Abu Bakr understood and continued; by analogy, understanding written text does not invalidate. Hadith from Sahih Bukhari... (2) The Prophet prayed carrying Umama and would put her down during sujud; that is a small action and does not invalidate. Hadith from Muslim... (3) If one eats more than a chickpea, prayer is invalid; athar from Abd al-Razzaq... (4) Passing does not cut off prayer; hadith from Abu Dawood... (5) Aisha said that animals and women do not cut prayer; she herself lay before the Prophet during prayer. (6) The passer commits a grave sin; hadith about delaying forty years. (7) Meaning forty years; hadith from Abu Huraira about delaying a hundred years.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with detailed proofs. The author presents three scenarios that do not invalidate prayer: (a) looking at writing and understanding it without speaking, based on the hadith of Abu Bakr receiving a gesture; (b) eating food stuck between teeth less than the size of a chickpea, considered like swallowing saliva; (c) someone passing in front of the person praying, which is sinful for the passer but does not invalidate the prayer. The author then provides multiple ahadith to support each ruling: the story of Abu Bakr (gesture), the Prophet carrying Umama (small action), and the prohibition of passing in front (sin but not invalidating). The text also warns that excessive action (like eating more than a chickpea) does invalidate. The commentary emphasizes that the prayer remains valid in these cases because the actions are minimal (qalil) and do not constitute excessive action (amal kathir). The sin of passing in front is severe, as indicated by the hadith about the passer being better off standing for forty years.

Key Terms
عمل کثیر (amal kathir): Excessive action that would invalidate prayer; the opposite is 'amal qalil' (small action). The text contrasts the small actions (looking, eating less than chickpea, passing) which are not excessive.
حمّصة (himmisa): A chickpea; used as a measure for the quantity of food that, if eaten in prayer, is considered negligible.
مار (marr): One who passes in front of the person praying; the action of passing (marur) is sinful but does not invalidate the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer becomes invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer remains valid because the amount is negligible.
C) The prayer is valid but requires prostration of forgetfulness (sajdah sahw).
D) The prayer becomes invalid only if the food is swallowed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, small actions (amal qalil) such as looking at writing without speaking, eating less than a chickpea, or someone passing in front do not invalidate the prayer.

Salah — Prayer

Minimal Extra Actions Do Not Invalidate Prayer
Ruling on minimal extra actions in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on minimal extra actions (ʿamal qalīl) in prayer and identify the prophetic evidence from the hadith of Umama bint Zainab.

Core Idea
Carrying a child during prayer is a minor action that does not invalidate the prayer, as proven by the Prophet's own practice.

Many assume that any extraneous movement during prayer automatically invalidates it. The Hanafi school, however, distinguishes between minor and major actions based on the prophetic example. In this lesson, we examine the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) carrying his granddaughter Umama during prayer and derive the principle that ʿamal qalīl is excused.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that a small amount of action during prayer does not invalidate it. The act is carrying a child (or similar minor movement) while praying. The ruling is that the prayer remains valid (sahih).
The ruling is a permission (rukhsa) based on a specific action of the Prophet.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Abu Qatada: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to pray while carrying Umama bint Zainab. When he stood up he would carry her, and when he prostrated he would put her down.' This is a Sunnah ahad report from Sahih Muslim.
The hadith is a fiʿl (action) of the Prophet, which in Hanafi usul indicates permissibility (ibaha) unless there is evidence of prohibition.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's carrying of his granddaughter during prayer shows that such an action is not inherently incompatible with prayer. Therefore, a small amount of extraneous movement does not invalidate the prayer. The illah (effective cause) is the minimal nature of the action (ʿamal qalīl).
The Hanafi school defines ʿamal qalīl by custom (ʿurf): whatever is considered little by people is excused.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Hanafi school holds this view in contrast to the Shafiʿi school, which generally considers any deliberate unnecessary movement to invalidate the prayer, unless it is very minor. However, the Hanafis are more permissive in quantity.
The practical difference: In Shafiʿi, adjusting one's garment slightly may require sajda as-sahw; in Hanafi, it does not.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The reliance on this hadith is strong because it is a clear action of the Prophet. Later Hanafi scholars (e.g., in al-Hidayah) maintain this ruling as mufta bihi. The distinction between ʿamal qalīl and ʿamal kathīr (three consecutive movements) is standardized in the school.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded on this principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل لا يفسد الصلوة مار في موضع السجود فاسد نہیں ہوتی اسکی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابی قتادة أن رسول الله الله كان يصلى و هو حامل أمامة بنت زينب صلى الله بنت رسول الله عله ، و ل و لابي العاص بن الربيع ، فاذا قام حملها و اذا سجدو ضعها ؟ قال يحى : قال مالک : نعم - (مسلم شریف ، باب جواز حمل الصبيان في الصلوة - وان الفعل التقليل لا يبطل الصلوۃ ص ۲۰۵، نمبر ۴۳ ۱۲۱۲٫۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ تھوڑے بہت عمل سے حضرت امامہ کو اٹھاتے تھے ، اسلئے اس سے نماز فاسد نہیں ہوئی لیکن عمل کثیر ہو تو نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
Section: That which does not invalidate prayer. When something passes in the place of prostration, the prayer does not become invalid. Its proof is this hadith: From Abu Qatada that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to pray while carrying Umama bint Zainab (the daughter of the Messenger of Allah) and for Abu al-As ibn al-Rabi'. When he stood up he would carry her, and when he prostrated he would put her down. Yahya said: Malik said: Yes. (Muslim, Chapter on the permissibility of carrying children in prayer - and that a small action does not invalidate prayer, p. 205, no. 43? 1212.5). In this hadith, with a small amount of action he would pick up Umama, therefore the prayer was not invalidated by that. But if there is a large amount of action, the prayer will become invalid.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage introduces a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding actions during prayer. The text states that minimal extra movements do not invalidate the prayer, citing the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) carried his granddaughter Umama during prayer. The Hanafi principle derived here is that only a large amount of extraneous action (ʿamal kathīr) invalidates the prayer; minor actions are excused. The ruling is classified as a concession (rukhsa) based on prophetic practice.

Key Terms
عمل قلیل (ʿamal qalīl): A small amount of extraneous movement during prayer that does not invalidate the prayer according to the majority of Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It invalidates the prayer only if the child is a girl.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits minimal extra actions (ʿamal qalīl) during prayer, relying on the Prophet's example of carrying his granddaughter.

Eating More Than a Chickpea Invalidates Prayer
Ruling that eating more than a chickpea invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating during prayer and explain the proof from the athar of 'Ata'.

Core Idea
Eating more than a chickpea invalidates prayer; less does not, but is makruh.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on eating during prayer. While some schools invalidate prayer for any consumption, the Hanafis set a threshold: a chickpea's amount. This ruling is based on the concept of major action (ʿamal kathīr) and is supported by an athar from ʿAtaʾ, reported in the Musannaf of ʿAbd al-Razzaq. Understanding this distinction is crucial for distinguishing between invalidating and disliked actions in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is eating or drinking during prayer. The ruling: if more than a chickpea, prayer becomes fasid; if less, valid but makruh.
The threshold is specific to the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the dalil
The source cites an athar from ʿAtaʾ: 'He does not eat nor drink while praying, and if he does, he repeats.'
This is a weak narration (zanni al-thubut) but used as evidence.
3
Analyse the istidlal
The command to repeat (aʿada) implies the original prayer was invalid. The prohibition of eating/drinking indicates it is contrary to the nature of prayer.
The Hanafi usul principle: any action that contradicts the state of prayer invalidates it if it is substantial.
4
Examine the ikhtilaf
Other schools (Shafi'i, etc.) hold that any eating invalidates, without quantity distinction. The Hanafi school uses the quantity to define major versus minor action.
The ikhtilaf is based on the definition of ʿamal kathīr.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: more than a chickpea invalidates; less than that does not but is makruh.
This is the position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

If one eats less than that, the prayer remains valid but is disliked. The ruling is based on the concept of ʿamal kathīr being that which distracts from the prayer.

Passing in Front of a Praying Person: Sin but Not Invalidation
Ruling with proof: passing does not invalidate but is sinful

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on passing in front of a praying person, cite the proof texts, and distinguish between the validity of the prayer and the sinfulness of the passer.

Core Idea
Passing in front of a praying person does not invalidate the prayer, but the passer commits a major sin.

A common assumption is that any person or animal passing in front of a worshipper invalidates the prayer. However, the Hanafi school, relying on explicit hadith, rules that the prayer remains perfectly valid. Instead, the sin falls on the passer for disrupting the sanctity of the prayer. This lesson examines the key narrations and their legal implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Clearly state the act: passing between a praying person and the qibla (or the place of prostration). The Hanafi ruling has two parts: the prayer is not invalidated; the passer is sinful.
The ruling on the prayer is sahih (valid); the ruling on the passer is makruh tahrimi (sinful).
2
Examine the primary proof
The hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'Nothing cuts off the prayer...' This directly negates invalidation. Understand the linguistic implication: 'la yaqta' is a general negation.
The hadith is ahad but accepted in fiqh.
3
Analyze the secondary proof from Aisha
The incident where Aisha lay between the Prophet and the qibla while he prayed. She slipped away without disturbing the prayer. This establishes that even a woman passing does not break the prayer.
Contrast with the Shafi'i position that a woman invalidates.
4
Understand the sin component
The hadith of Abu Juhaym states that 40 (days/months/years) of standing would be better than passing. The hadith of Abu Huraira mentions 100 years. This emphasizes the severity of the sin.
The minimum is 40 years according to the preferred interpretation.

Commentary
This passage collects multiple narrations to establish the Hanafi ruling that passing in front of a praying person does not invalidate the prayer of either the passer or the one praying, but the passer commits a major sin. The hadith from Abu Saʿid indicates that nothing 'cuts off' the prayer. The incident with ʿAʾisha shows that even a woman passing does not invalidate. The extent of sin is emphasized: it would be better to stand for forty years than to pass. The Hanafi school thus distinguishes between invalidation (which does not occur) and sinfulness (which does).

Looking at Divorcée's Private Parts: Prayer and Reconciliation
Ruling with reasoning on two areas of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at a divorcée's private parts during prayer and explain why it does not invalidate prayer but does effect reconciliation.

Core Idea
Looking with desire: valid prayer but established ruj'a.

This passage presents a nuanced ruling that straddles two domains of fiqh: prayer (ṣalāh) and divorce (ṭalāq). The same action—looking at one's divorced wife's private parts with desire—is evaluated differently depending on the legal context. Understanding the distinct criteria for each ruling (invalidating prayer vs. effecting rujʿa) is essential for applying the correct ḥukm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Context
Locate the specific action being ruled upon: a man looking at the private parts (farj) of his divorced wife (al-muṭallaqah) with desire (bi-shahwah) while he is performing prayer.
The act occurs during prayer, which is already in progress.
2
Determine the Ruling for Prayer
Extract the ruling: 'lā tafsidu' (it does not invalidate). Note that the text emphasizes that this is the chosen (mukhtār) opinion.
The aṣl (basis) for invalidation is either major extraneous action (ʿamal kathīr) or impurity exiting the body—neither is present here.
3
Analyze Why Prayer Remains Valid
Understand the reasoning: looking is not a major action and does not involve impurity. Prayer is only invalidated by specific factors (mufsidāt), not by every distraction.
The Hanafi school limits invalidation to clear textual or qiyās-based factors.
4
Determine the Ruling for Reconciliation
Identify the second ruling: 'thabata bihi al-rajʿa' (reconciliation is established). This means the husband’s action constitutes a valid rujʿa, returning the wife to the marriage without a new contract.
Rujʿa is established by any action that clearly indicates the husband’s intention to resume marital relations.
5
Understand Why Reconciliation is Established
Note the legal basis: according to the Hanafis, looking at the inside of the private part (farj al-dākhil) triggers the prohibition of affinity (ḥurmat muṣāhara), which is akin to actual marital intimacy. This action thus signals reconciliation.
The same act has different consequences in prayer and divorce law because the controlling principles (ʿamal kathīr vs. indication of rujʿa) are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۵۵۱) لا تفسد وان أثِمَ المارّ ولا تفسد بنظره الى فرج المطلقة بشهوة في المختار وان ثبت به الرجعة. ترجمه : (۵۵۱) اور اپنی مطلقہ عورت کی شرمگاہ کو شہوت کی نظر سے دیکھنا مختار مذہب میں نماز کو فاسد نہیں کرتا، اگر چہ اس سے رجعت ثابت ہو جائے گی۔ تشریح : نماز فاسد ہونے کا مدار عمل کثیر ہے، یا نجاست کا نکلنا ہے عورت کی شرمگاہ دیکھنے کی وجہ سے نہ عمل کثیر ہوا اور نہ مصلی کے بدن سے نجاست نکلی اس لئے نماز فاسد نہیں ہوگی۔ اور رجعت کا مدار ایسی حرکت کرنا ہے جو دلالت کرتی ہو کہ اس نے رجعت کی ، یا حرمت مصاہرہ کو دعوت دیتی ہو، اور فرج داخل کے دیکھنے سے حنفیہ کے نزدیک حرمت مصاہرہ ثابت ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے رجعت ہو جاۓ گی۔
(551) It does not invalidate, even if the passer sins, and it does not invalidate due to his looking at the private part of his divorced wife with desire according to the chosen (muhtar) opinion, even if reconciliation (rujʿa) is established by it. Translation: (551) And looking at the private part of his divorced wife with desire does not invalidate the prayer in the chosen madhhab, even if reconciliation is established by it. Clarification: The basis for invalidation of prayer is major action (ʿamal kathīr) or excretion of impurity. Looking at the wife's private part is neither major action nor does impurity exit from the body, so the prayer is not invalidated. And the basis for reconciliation is an action that indicates he intended reconciliation or invites (the effect of) the prohibition of affinity (ḥurmat muṣāhara). According to the Hanafis, looking at the inside of the private part establishes the prohibition of affinity, so reconciliation occurs.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced ruling: looking at one's divorced wife's private parts with desire during prayer does not invalidate the prayer, because it is not a major extraneous action nor does it involve impurity. However, such an act can effect a legal reconciliation (rujʿa) in divorce law because the Hanafi school considers that seeing the inside of the private part establishes a prohibition of affinity (muṣāhara), which is tantamount to a manifestation of the husband's intention to take her back. Thus the same act has different effects in prayer and in divorce.

Key Terms
رجعت (rujʿa): The return to marital relations after a revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī) without a new marriage contract.
شهوة (shahwa): Sexual desire; a condition for the act to effect rujʿa according to the text.
عمل كثير (ʿamal kathīr): A major extraneous action that invalidates prayer, defined as actions that would lead a spectator to believe the person is not praying.

Introduction to Makruhat in Prayer
Introduction to the chapter on disliked acts during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define makruh in Hanafi fiqh, list three examples of makruh actions in prayer from the text, and explain why they do not invalidate the prayer.

Core Idea
Makruh actions in prayer do not break it, but they reduce its reward and can be sinful.

The Hanafi school enumerates 77 actions that are disliked (makruh) during prayer. While these actions do not invalidate the prayer, they diminish its perfection and may incur sin depending on severity. This lesson introduces the concept of makruhat and their legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Legal Category of Makruh
Recognize that 'makruh' in Hanafi fiqh is an action that is disliked but not haram or invalidating. There are two subcategories: tahrimi (close to haram) and tanzihi (less severe).
Makruh tahrimi is sinful if done deliberately; makruh tanzihi is not sinful but best avoided.
2
Identify the Examples Given in the Text
From the passage, note specific actions listed: cracking knuckles, interlacing fingers, placing hands on hips, turning the neck, removing pebbles more than once, playing with body or clothes, and intentionally leaving a wajib or sunnah.
The list is representative of the 77 disliked actions.
3
Determine the Ruling on Validity
Understand that doing any of these makruh actions does not invalidate the prayer. The prayer remains valid (sahih) but complete reward is diminished.
This is a key distinction from mufsidat (invalidators) which break the prayer.
4
Apply the Subcategory Distinction
For each example, consider whether it is likely makruh tahrimi or tanzihi based on its nature. Intentional omission of a wajib may be tahrimi, while minor fidgeting is tanzihi.
The text does not specify, so scholars have assigned categories based on severity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : (يكره للمصلي سبعة وسبعون شيئا . ..... واجب یا سنت کا قصدا چھوڑنا .... اور اپنے بدن یا کپڑے سے کھیلنا سجدہ کے لئے ایک مرتبہ سے زیادہ سنگریزے ہٹانا ، انگلیاں چٹخانا انگلیوں کو انگلیوں میں ڈالنا کوکھ پر ہاتھ رکھنا گردن پھرا کر دیکھنا

Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on makruhat (disliked acts) in prayer. The Hanafi school enumerates 77 actions that are makruh (either tanzihi or tahrimi). The text lists a few examples: intentionally omitting a wajib or sunnah, playing with body/clothes, clearing the place of prostration more than once, cracking knuckles, interlacing fingers, akhth bi-marfiq (hands on hips), and turning the neck. The key point is that these actions do not invalidate the prayer but reduce its reward or are sinful if done deliberately (especially makruh tahrimi).

Key Terms
مکروہ (makrūh): An action that is disliked in prayer, either tahriman (close to haram) or tanzihan (less severe). Doing it does not invalidate the prayer.

Key Takeaway

8 min
Ruling on Looking at Divorced Wife's Private Parts During Prayer
States ruling that prayer is not invalidated by such looking, though it establishes return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at a divorced wife's private parts during prayer and explain why it does not invalidate the prayer.

Core Idea
Prayer remains valid even when looking with desire at a divorced wife's private parts, though it establishes the right of return.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario that tests the boundary between valid prayer and invalidating actions. The Hanafi school's preferred (mukhtar) position distinguishes between the domain of prayer validity and the domain of marital relations. Let us examine the ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is looking at the private parts of one's divorced wife with desire during prayer. According to the Hanafi mukhtar, prayer is not invalidated (la tafsidu).
2
Understand the legal basis for non-invalidation
Nullification of prayer depends on either excessive action (amal kathir) or exiting of impurity. Looking does not involve either; it is a minor action and does not release impurity.
This is based on the general principle that only major acts or impurity nullify prayer.
3
Note the concurrent effect: return (raj'ah) is established
Even though the prayer remains valid, this look by the husband indicates his desire to return to the marriage during the waiting period, thus establishing raj'ah.
Raj'ah does not require a new contract; any act indicating intent suffices.
4
Distinguish between sinfulness and legal effect on prayer
The act may be sinful (especially if done with desire outside marriage), but sinfulness does not automatically render prayer invalid. The two are separate legal considerations.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the ruling on the act itself and its effect on worship.

فصل ما يكره للمصلى (۵۵۱) لا تفسد وان أثِمَ المارّ ولا تفسد بنظره الى فرج المطلقة بشهوة في المختار وان ثبت به الرجعة. ماجة شریف، باب المرور بین یدی المصلی ۱۳۴۰، نمبر ۹۴۶) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ سو سال ٹھہرار ہے اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ بخاری والی سوسال حدیث میں چالیس سال مراد ہے۔ ترجمه : (۵۵۱) اور اپنی مطلقہ عورت کی شرمگاہ کو شہوت کی نظر سے دیکھنا مختار مذہب میں نماز کو فاسد نہیں کرتا، اگر چہ اس سے رجعت ثابت ہو جائے گی۔ تشریح : نماز فاسد ہونے کا مدار عمل کثیر ہے، یا نجاست کا نکلنا ہے عورت کی شرمگاہ دیکھنے کی وجہ سے نہ عمل کثیر ہوا اور نہ مصلی کے بدن سے نجاست نکلی اس لئے نماز فاسد نہیں ہوگی۔ اور رجعت کا مدار ایسی حرکت کرنا ہے جو دلالت کرتی ہو کہ اس نے رجعت کی ، یا حرمت مصاہرہ کو دعوت دیتی ہو، اور فرج داخل کے دیکھنے سے حنفیہ کے نزدیک حرمت مصاہرہ ثابت ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے رجعت ہو جاۓ گی۔
Prayer is not invalidated even if the passer-by sins, and it is not invalidated by his looking at the private parts of his divorced wife with desire according to the chosen school, even if return is established by it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: prayer remains valid even if one looks at the private parts of one's divorced wife with desire, according to the preferred position (mukhtar) of the Hanafi school. Although this look establishes the right of return (رجعة) to the marriage, it does not nullify the prayer. The reason is that nullification of prayer depends on excessive action (amal kathir) or exiting of impurity. Looking does not constitute excessive action, nor does it release impurity. The return is established because looking at the private parts with desire invokes the prohibition of affinity (حرمت مصاہرہ), which is sufficient to indicate the intent of return. The passage also references a hadith about passing in front of a worshiper, but the main focus is on the ruling about looking.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The right of the husband to return to his divorced wife during her waiting period without a new marriage contract.
مختار (mukhtar): The preferred or chosen position within the Hanafi school for issuing fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mukhtar position, what is the ruling on looking at the private parts of one's divorced wife with desire during prayer?
A) Prayer is invalidated because looking with desire is a major sin.
B) Prayer is not invalidated, but it establishes the return (رجعة).
C) Prayer is invalidated only if the look is prolonged.
D) Prayer becomes makruh but remains valid.

Key Takeaway
Looking at a divorced wife's private parts with desire does not invalidate prayer, but it establishes the husband's right of return.

Classification of Disliked Acts in Prayer
Definition of makruh types and start of the list

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi, and identify several specific disliked acts from the Hanafi list.

Core Idea
Disliked acts do not invalidate prayer but reduce reward; they are either severely or mildly disliked.

This passage opens the section on disliked acts (makruhat) in prayer, defining two categories and starting a list of seventy-seven items. Understanding these distinctions helps the worshiper avoid actions that diminish the quality of prayer without breaking it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of makruh
The passage explains two levels: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, near haram) and makruh tanzihi (mildly disliked). Both reduce reward but do not invalidate the prayer.
This is a key Hanafi distinction; other schools may not differentiate.
2
Note the effect on prayer validity
Committing a makruh act does not make the prayer invalid (fasid or batil); it remains valid but with reduced reward.
3
Recognize the total count
The author explicitly states there are 77 disliked acts for the worshiper. This lesson covers the first portion of that list.
4
Memorize the first set of items
The list begins with intentional omission of wajib or sunnah, then includes physical actions like cracking fingers, interlocking fingers, resting hands on hips, turning the neck, and various clothing-related gestures.
Pay attention to specific actions; many are common distractions.
5
Appreciate the thoroughness
The list covers posture, garment handling, recitation, and even yawning. This comprehensive enumeration helps the worshiper refine his focus.

Section: Concerning disliked things for the worshiper. Necessary note: Disliked is of two types: 1) Makruh Tahrimi: close to haram, severe dislike. 2) Makruh Tanzihi: lesser dislike, but still not good. Committing disliked acts in prayer does not invalidate it, though it is not good. For the worshiper, seventy-seven things are disliked: intentionally leaving a wajib or sunnah, playing with one's body or clothes, removing pebbles more than once for prostration, cracking fingers, interlocking fingers, placing hands on hips, turning the neck to look, sitting like a dog, spreading both hands in prostration, rolling up sleeves with both hands, praying only in a lower garment when able to wear a shirt, responding to salam with a gesture, sitting on both buttocks without excuse, a man tying his hair in a bun, wearing a used turban with the end hanging open in the middle, gathering the garment, leaving both ends of the garment hanging, inserting hands into garment such that they cannot come out, taking garment out from the right armpit and placing both ends on the left shoulder, reciting Qur'an in a state other than standing, lengthening the first rak'ah in nafl, lengthening the second rak'ah over the first in all prayers, reciting a makruh surah in one rak'ah of fard, reciting a higher surah, interrupting between two surahs with a small surah in two rak'ahs, smelling perfume, fanning oneself with garment or fan once or twice, turning fingers or toes away from qibla in prostration etc., leaving the hands on the knees in ruku', yawning.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the disliked acts (makruhat) in prayer. It first explains that makruh is of two types: tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, close to haram) and tanzihi (mildly disliked). Importantly, committing any of these disliked acts does not invalidate the prayer, though it is blameworthy. Then it begins listing the seventy-seven disliked acts. The list includes intentional omission of wajib or sunnah, playing with the body or clothes, removing pebbles more than once for prostration, cracking fingers, interlocking fingers, placing hands on hips, turning the neck, sitting in certain postures, folding clothes, responding to salam with gesture, and many others. The list serves as a memorization anchor for students to know which actions to avoid in prayer while understanding that they do not break the prayer.

Key Terms
مکروہ تحریمی (makruh tahrimi): An action that is prohibitively disliked, close to haram, and should be avoided; committing it is sinful but does not invalidate prayer.
مکروہ تنزیہی (makruh tanzihi): An action that is mildly disliked; avoiding it is better, but committing it is not sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Makruh acts in prayer are of two types (tahrimi and tanzihi) and do not invalidate the prayer; the list of 77 helps worshiper avoid them.

Makruh Acts in Salah (Prayer)
List of disliked actions in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain at least 10 actions considered makruh in salah according to the Hanafi school and categorise them (e.g., posture, clothing, recitation, environment).

Core Idea
Makruh acts reduce reward but do not invalidate the prayer.

The Hanafi fiqh literature contains an extensive list of actions that are disliked (makruh) during salah. These actions, while not rendering the prayer invalid, detract from its perfection and the worshipper's khushu' (concentration). Understanding these rulings helps the Muslim avoid them and pray with greater focus.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of makruh
Differentiate between makruh tanzihi (disliked) and makruh tahrimi (prohibited-disliked); most items here are tanzihi.
Haram actions invalidate or are sinful; makruh actions are lesser in severity.
2
Categorise the list
Group the listed items into categories such as posture, clothing, recitation, environment, and internal distractions.
This helps in memorisation and application.
3
Identify the underlying principle
Most makruh actions disturb khushu' or contradict the sunnah of the Prophet ﷺ.
The principle is to avoid anything that compromises the spirit of prayer.
4
Note exceptions and boundaries
Some actions, like catching a louse, can become amal kathir (excessive action) if repeated, which invalidates prayer.
Amal qalil is merely makruh; amal kathir is mubtil.
5
Apply to your own prayer
Reflect on your daily prayer and identify any of these makruhat that may occur, then resolve to avoid them.
This turns knowledge into practice.

Section on what is disliked for the one praying. [List includes:] Sitting on one's heels, placing both hands down in prostration, rolling up sleeves with both hands, praying only in a loincloth while able to wear a shirt, responding to salaam with a gesture, sitting on four limbs without excuse, a man tying his hair, wrapping a used turban on the head and leaving the middle of the head uncovered, rolling up the cloth, letting both edges of the cloth hang down, inserting the hand into the cloth such that it cannot come out, taking the cloth from under the right armpit and placing both ends on the left shoulder, reciting Qur'an in other than standing posture in nafl? Prolonging the first rak'ah? Prolonging the second rakah more than the first in all prayers, reciting an additional surah in one rak'ah of fard, reciting a surah above [a previously recited surah], leaving a gap of one small surah between two surahs in two rak'ahs, smelling perfume, fanning oneself once or twice with cloth or fan, turning the fingers of hands and feet away from the qibla in prostration etc., and leaving placing both hands on the knees in ruku', yawning. ... Closing the eyes, raising the eyes to the sky, stretching, performing a small action (qalil), catching a louse, killing a louse, covering the nose and mouth, putting something in the mouth that prevents recitation, prostrating on the peak of the turban, prostrating on a picture, restricting oneself to the forehead without an excuse of nose ailment, praying on the road, in the bathhouse, in the toilet, in the cemetery, praying on someone's land without permission, praying near impurity, praying while suppressing urine, stool, or wind, praying with non-preventing impurity, praying in ordinary clothes, praying with head uncovered without humility, praying while food is present and desired, praying with something that distracts the heart and prevents khushu', rolling up sleeves with the hand, counting tasbih in prayer, the imam standing alone in the mihrab or a high place or a specific place. Section on what is disliked for the one praying.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a list of makruh (disliked) actions in salah according to the Hanafi school. The section is titled 'Fasl ma yukrahu li'l-musalli' (Section on what is disliked for the one praying). These actions are generally considered makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful) unless specified otherwise. They do not invalidate the prayer but reduce its reward and perfection. The list covers various categories: posture and movements (e.g., sitting on heels, placing hands down in sajdah, rolling up sleeves), clothing (e.g., wearing only a loincloth, leaving head uncovered without humility), recitation (e.g., reciting Qur'an in non-standing posture, adding extra surahs), interruptions (e.g., responding to salam with gesture, stretching, yawning, closing eyes, looking at the sky), environment (e.g., praying on road, bathhouse, toilet, cemetery, near impurity, on usurped land), and internal states (e.g., praying while suppressing need to relieve oneself, while food is present). The Hanafi school emphasizes khushu' (concentration) in prayer, and these acts distract from it. Some items like catching and killing lice are explicitly disliked because they constitute excessive action (amal kathir) which can invalidate prayer if done repeatedly. The list ends with the imam standing alone in the mihrab or a high place, which is disliked because it separates him from the congregation. All these rulings are based on the principle that prayer should be performed with dignity and focus.

Key Terms
مكروه (makruh): An action that is disliked in prayer, not sinful but against the preferred practice, reducing reward.
عمل قلیل (amal qalil): A small action performed during prayer, such as moving a hand once; it is makruh but does not invalidate prayer.
خشوع (khushu'): Humility and focus in prayer, which is disturbed by these disliked actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered makruh in prayer according to this passage?
A) Prostrating on the edge of the turban
B) Wearing a shirt and loincloth
C) Reciting Qur'an in standing posture
D) Keeping the hands on the knees in ruku'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school lists many actions as makruh in prayer; while they do not invalidate, avoiding them maximizes reward and khushu'.

Disliked Acts in Prayer: Understanding Makruh Tahrimi and Tanzihi
Lists disliked actions in prayer with detailed rulings on two items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi in prayer, and state the consequence of each (obligation of sajdah al-sahw or not).

Core Idea
Deliberately leaving a wajib is makruh tahrimi and requires sajdah al-sahw; leaving a sunnah is makruh tanzihi without sajdah.

In this passage from Thamaratun Najah, we encounter a comprehensive list of disliked acts (makruhat) in prayer, ranging from fidgeting to praying in inappropriate places. The author then zooms in on two critical items: the ruling on deliberately omitting a wajib or sunnah, and the prohibition of unnecessary movements. Understanding these distinctions — especially the difference between makruh tahrimi (prohibited-adjacent) and makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) — is essential for proper prayer discipline in the Hanafi school. This lesson will guide you through the analytical breakdown of these rulings and their evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Scan the list of makruhat and note which involve omission of obligations (wajib/sunnah) versus physical movements.
The passage is a long list; focus on items (552) and (553) for detail.
2
Analyze item (552): Deliberate omission
Read the translation and explanation: deliberately leaving a wajib makes sajdah al-sahw wajib and is makruh tahrimi; leaving a sunnah is makruh tanzihi without sajdah.
Key distinction: wajib vs. sunnah triggers different rulings.
3
Analyze item (553): Fidgeting
Examine the list: playing with clothes, moving pebbles, cracking fingers, etc. All are makruh, and the evidence from Quran and Hadith is provided.
Note the exception: one pebble movement for prostration is permissible.
4
Differentiate makruh tahrimi and tanzihi
Contrast the two categories: tahrimi is closer to haram (sinful, but prayer valid if wajib omitted? Actually prayer remains valid with sajdah), tanzihi is lightly disliked (no sin).
Use the dalil_block to see how the hadith proves the ruling for fidgeting.
5
Apply the distinction to new cases
Consider a case like cracking fingers in prayer: is it tahrimi or tanzihi? Based on the text, it is makruh (likely tanzihi) and does not require sajdah.
The absence of a specific 'tahrimi' label in item (553) suggests tanzihi.

آنکھیں بند رکھنا

(553) Playing with his clothes and body, and moving pebbles except for one prostration. 4 Standing behind when there is space in the row. 54 55 Wearing clothes with pictures. 56 Having a picture on the head, behind, front, or opposite, or praying in front of an oven or fireplace with fire. 58 Praying in front of sleeping people, or cleaning non-ceremonial dirt from the forehead in prayer. Designating a surah such that he does not recite other than it. It is disliked for the worshiper not to place a sutrah in front of him where people are likely to pass. Note: Bed could not be done. 61. The disliked acts. Translation (552): Deliberately leaving an obligatory or sunnah act. Explanation: If he leaves an obligatory act, sajdah al-sahw becomes necessary, but the prayer does not become invalid, and deliberately leaving it is makruh tahrimi. And if he leaves a sunnah, sajdah al-sahw is not necessary, and deliberately leaving it is makruh. Translation (553): And playing with his body or clothes, moving pebbles more than once for prostration, cracking fingers, interlocking fingers, placing hands on the waist. Explanation: Playing with clothes in prayer is disliked. Repeatedly moving pebbles is disliked, but if there is a necessity, moving a pebble once from the place of prostration is permissible. Cracking fingers in prayer is disliked. Interlocking fingers is disliked. And placing hands on the waist is also disliked. Evidence: (1) There should be humility and submission in prayer. The verse says: 'Stand before Allah with devotion' (Quran 2:238). Therefore, playing with the body and clothes is disliked. (2) There is also a hadith: From Ibn Abbas from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold hair or garment' (Bukhari, chapter on not folding garment in prayer, p.113, no.816; Muslim, chapter on limbs of prostration and prohibition of folding hair and garment, p.193, no.490). From this hadith it is understood that repeatedly moving the clothes without reason.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (makruhat) in prayer from the Hanafi school. It first lists various disliked actions, then provides a detailed commentary on two specific items: (552) deliberately leaving a wajib or sunnah, and (553) fidgeting with clothes or body. The author explains that leaving a wajib deliberately requires sajdah al-sahw and is makruh tahrimi, while leaving a sunnah is makruh tanzihi without sajdah al-sahw. For fidgeting, the author prohibits actions like cracking fingers, interlocking fingers, and placing hands on the waist, supporting this with Quranic emphasis on khushu' (2:238) and a hadith from Bukhari and Muslim prohibiting folding hair or garment during prostration. The passage reflects the Hanafi precision in categorizing disliked acts into tahrimi (prohibited-adjacent) and tanzihi (lightly disliked).

Key Terms
مکروہ تحریمی (makruh tahrimi): An action closer to haram; deliberately omitting a wajib incurs sin but does not invalidate prayer.
مکروہ تنزیہی (makruh tanzihi): Lightly disliked; leaving a sunnah deliberately is makruh tanzihi without requiring sajdah al-sahw.
سجدہ سہو (sajdah al-sahw): Prostration of forgetfulness required when a wajib is omitted or delayed, or a sunnah is mistakenly altered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if someone deliberately leaves a wajib during prayer?
A) The prayer becomes invalid (fasid).
B) Sajdah al-sahw becomes necessary, and the act is makruh tahrimi.
C) No compensation is needed; it is merely makruh tanzihi.
D) The prayer must be repeated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi prayer, disliked actions (makruhat) do not invalidate prayer, but leaving a wajib requires sajdah al-sahw while leaving a sunnah does not.

Intentionally Leaving a Wajib or Sunnah in Salah
Ruling on intentionally omitting a wajib or sunnah in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for intentionally omitting a wajib vs. a sunnah in salah and identify the required compensation.

Core Idea
A wajib omission requires sajda sahw but does not invalidate; a sunnah omission requires no compensation but is disliked.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on intentionally leaving a wajib or sunnah act in salah. Based on the text from Thamaratun Najah, we will clarify the consequences and the level of legal condemnation for each. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper prayer practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Determine whether the omitted act is categorized as wajib or sunnah in the Hanafi school. This affects the ruling and required compensation.
Wajib is between fard and sunnah; examples include the first tashahhud.
2
Determine sajda sahw requirement
For a wajib omission, sajda sahw becomes necessary (wajib). For a sunnah omission, no sajda sahw is required.
Sajda sahw compensates for the missing wajib without invalidating the prayer.
3
Identify the hukm level
Intentionally leaving a wajib is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Intentionally leaving a sunnah is makruh tanzih (lightly disliked).
Makruh tahrimi is closer to haram; makruh tanzih is permissible but discouraged.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools may consider wajib equivalent to fard, making omission invalidating. The Hanafi school maintains the distinction.
This difference stems from differing definitions of wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل ما يكره للمصلى (۵۵۲) ترک واجب او سنة عمدا
ترجمه : (۵۵۲) واجب یا سنت کا قصدا چھوڑنا۔
تشريح : واجب چھوڑے گا تو سجدہ سہو لازم ہوگا، البتہ نماز فاسد نہیں ہوگی، اور جان کر چھوڑ نا مکرہ تحریمی ہے۔ اور سنت چھوڑے گا تو سجدہ سہو لازم نہیں ہوگا ، اور جان کر چھوڑ نا مکروہ ہے۔
Chapter: What is disliked for the one praying. (552) Intentionally leaving a wajib or sunnah. Translation: (552) Leaving a wajib or sunnah intentionally. Explanation: If he leaves a wajib, sajda sahw becomes necessary, however the prayer does not become invalid, and leaving it knowingly is makruh tahrimi. If he leaves a sunnah, sajda sahw does not become necessary, and leaving it knowingly is makruh.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This is a ruling statement about the consequences of intentionally omitting a wajib or sunnah in salah. The text distinguishes between wajib and sunnah: omitting a wajib triggers the requirement for sajda sahw (prostration of forgetfulness) but does not invalidate the prayer; however, it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Omitting a sunnah does not require sajda sahw and is only makruh (generally disliked, i.e., makruh tanzih). The ruling emphasizes that wajib is a category between fard and sunnah in the Hanafi school, and its omission requires compensation but not repetition.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib): A necessary act in salah that is less than fard; omission requires sajda sahw but does not invalidate the prayer.
سنة (sunnah): Recommended act; omission does not require sajda sahw but is disliked (makruh) if done intentionally.
سجدہ سہو (sajda al-sahw): Two prostrations performed at the end of salah to compensate for errors, such as omission of a wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if someone intentionally omits a wajib in salah?
A) The prayer becomes invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid but sajda sahw is required, and the act is makruh tahrimi.
C) Nothing is required; the prayer is still perfect.
D) Only sajda sahw is required, and the act is halal.

Key Takeaway
Intentionally omitting a wajib in salah requires sajda sahw and is makruh tahrimi; omitting a sunnah is merely makruh with no required compensation.

Disliked Actions in Salah: Fidgeting and Related Acts
List and proof of various makruh actions in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on six disliked actions in salah and cite the specific hadith evidence for each.

Core Idea
Fidgeting in salah contradicts khushu'; the Hanafi school rules it makruh tanzih with a one-time exception for necessity.

Salah requires humility and stillness. The Prophet ﷺ explicitly forbade certain restless behaviors that detract from devotion. This passage from the Hanafi text lists those actions—playing with clothes or body, repeatedly moving pebbles, snapping or interlocking fingers, and standing with hands on hips—and supports each with clear hadith. Understanding these rulings helps us perfect our prayer and avoid distractions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions and their ruling
List the six specific actions mentioned and note that the author states each is 'makruh'. Recognize that the overall category is 'abath (fidgeting).
The Arabic phrase 'لا اكف شعرا ولا ثوبا' indicates the prohibition of folding back hair or clothing.
2
Locate the primary evidence (dalil)
For each action, find the hadith or athar cited. For example, the prohibition of finger snapping is from Ali (ra): 'لا تفقع اصابعك وانت في الصلوة'.
Multiple ahadith are cited; identify which hadith supports which action.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof supports the ruling)
Explain how the linguistic form (e.g., imperative, prohibition) indicates dislike. For instance, 'نهى' (forbade) shows prohibition, but the Hanafi school interprets it as karahah tanzih because exceptions exist (one-time pebble removal).
The hadith on pebbles explicitly allows one movement, confirming the ruling is not absolute.
4
Note the exception and its scope
The removal of pebbles is allowed once if necessary. This exception shows that not all movement is prohibited; only excessive or playful fidgeting is makruh.
The exception applies only to the place of prostration and only when needed.
5
Apply the principle to modern contexts
Analogous actions today include adjusting a watch, playing with a beard, or repeatedly shifting position. The ruling remains makruh tanzih.
Essential movements (e.g., adjusting for pain) are exempt under the general principle of necessity.

(مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب مسح الحصی ، ج ثانی ص ۳۹ نمبر ۲۴۰۶) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کنکری کو الٹنا پلٹنا ہو تو صرف ایک مرتبہ کر سکتے ہو ۔ (6) انگلیاں چٹانا مکروہ ہے اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن على ان رسول الله عليه قال لا تفقع اصابعك وانت في الصلوة . (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب ما يكره في الصلوۃ ص ۱۳۶، نمبر ۹۶۴ و سنن البیھقی ، باب كراهية تفقيع الاصابع في الصلوة ، ج ثانی، ص ۴۱۰ نمبر ۳۵۷۴) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ نماز میں انگلیاں چٹخانا مکروہ ہے۔ (۷) کوکھ پر ہاتھ رکھنا مکروہ ہے اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابی ھریر ة عن النبی صلى الله عليه وسلم انه نهى ان يصلى الرجل مختصرا . (مسلم شریف، باب كراهية الاختصار في الصلوۃ ص ۲۰۶، نمبر ۵۱۲۱۸ و ابوداؤد شریف، باب النخصر والا قعاء ، ص ۱۳۷، نمبر ۹۰۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کو کھ پر ہاتھ رکھنے سے حضور نے منع فرمایا ہے۔ (۸) عن زياد بن صبيح الحنفى قال : صليت الى جنب ابن عمر فوضعت يدى على خاصرتی، فلما صلى قال : هذا الصلب في الصلوة، و كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ينهى عنه (ابوداود شریف، باب التخصر والا قعاء ، ص ۱۳۸، نمبر ۹۰۳) اس حدیث سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ کو کچھ پر ہاتھ رکھنا مکروہ ہے۔
لغت : العبث کھیلنا۔ قلب الحصے کنکری کو الٹ پلٹ کرنا۔ فرقعہ : چٹانا، فرقعة الاصابع : انگلیوں کو چٹیا نا ۔ تشبیک : شبک سے مشتق ہے، انگلیوں کو انگلیوں میں ڈالنا۔ التخصر : کوکھ پر ہاتھ رکھنا۔ ماخذ ہے خاصرة، کوکھ۔
(553) And fidgeting with his garment and his body, and turning over pebbles except for prostration once, and snapping fingers, and interlocking them, (554) and placing hands on hips, and turning with his neck, and squatting, and spreading forearms, and rolling up sleeves. Translation: (553) And playing with one's body or clothes, removing pebbles more than once for prostration, snapping fingers, interlocking fingers, placing hands on hips. Explanation: Playing with clothes is makruh. Repeatedly removing pebbles is makruh, but if necessary, one time is permissible. Snapping fingers is makruh. Interlocking fingers is makruh. Placing hands on hips is makruh. Proofs: (1) Humility is required. (2) Hadith: Ibn Abbas reported the Prophet said: I was commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold back hair or clothing. (3) Athar: al-Thawri said traditions come that fidgeting is disliked. (4) Hadith: Mu'ayqib asked about removing pebbles: if necessary, one time. (5) Another hadith: stand for prayer, do not remove pebbles, for mercy faces you. (6) Hadith: Ali: do not snap fingers in prayer. (7) Hadith: Abu Huraira: Prophet forbade praying with hands on hips. (8) Ibn Umar said Prophet forbade that. Lexicon: al-'abath: fidgeting; qalb al-hasa: turning pebbles; farqa'ah: snapping; tashbīk: interlocking; takhassur: hands on hips.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage enumerates several makruh (disliked) actions during salah and provides proofs from Quran, hadith, and the practice of the salaf. The author first lists the actions, then gives a translation, then a detailed explanation, and finally a list of proofs (wajh). The proofs demonstrate that each action is makruh due to the requirement of khushu' (humility) and explicit prohibitions. Key hadith include the command to prostrate on seven bones without folding clothing, hadith on removing pebbles only once, forbidding finger snapping, and the prohibition of akhtisar (hands on hips). The lexicon clarifies terms. The overall ruling is that these actions are makruh tanzih (mildly disliked), as they contradict the spirit of devotion.

Key Terms
العبث (al-'abath): Fidgeting or playing with one's body or clothes during prayer, considered makruh.
قلب الحصى (qalb al-ḥaṣā): Moving or turning pebbles on the ground during prayer; allowed only once for necessity.
فرقعة الأصابع (farqa'at al-aṣābi'): Snapping or cracking the fingers, which is makruh in salah.
التخصر (al-takhṣur): Placing hands on the hips (akhtisar), which is explicitly forbidden in hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith from Mu'ayqib, how many times can a person remove pebbles from the place of prostration without being considered makruh?
A) None, it is always forbidden.
B) Once, if necessary.
C) Up to three times.
D) Unlimited times.

Key Takeaway
Minor unnecessary movements in salah are makruh tanzih; the exception for necessity allows a one-time action.

Salah — Prayer
beginner
6 min
Turning Neck, Squatting, Spreading Arms, and Rolling Sleeves in Salah
Rulings on additional disliked postures and actions in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify makruh actions in salah and distinguish between actions that invalidate prayer and those that merely incur dislike.

Core Idea
Turning the neck alone is makruh; turning the chest invalidates prayer.

In this lesson, we examine several actions that are disliked (makruh) in salah but do not break the prayer unless the chest turns from the qibla. The source passage from Thamaratun Najah lists these actions and explains the conditions under which they are merely disliked versus invalidating.

Guided Path
1
Identify Each Action and Its Ruling
Read the text and list the four actions: turning the neck, sitting in iq'a', spreading arms in sujud, and rolling sleeves. Note that each is described as "makruh" (disliked).
The text explicitly uses the term 'makruh' for each.
2
Understand the Threshold for Invalidation
Focus on the condition for turning the neck: if the chest also turns from the qibla, the prayer becomes invalid (fasid). If only the neck turns, it remains valid but makruh.
This is a key distinction: the chest orientation is decisive.
3
Analyze the Rationale for Dislike
For iq'a', the text says it resembles a dog. For spreading arms, it is the posture of a dog. For rolling sleeves, it is unnecessary fidgeting. Understanding the rationale helps remember the rulings.
Resemblance to animals and unnecessary movement are reasons for dislike.
4
Note Cross-Madhhab Differences
The key term 'al-iq'ā' notes that some schools consider it a valid sitting position; the Hanafi school considers it makruh tanzih. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
Acknowledging valid differences strengthens fiqh understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۵۵۴) گردن پھرا کر دیکھنا، اکڑ و بیٹھنا، سجدے میں دونوں ہاتھ بچھا دینا، دونوں ہاتھوں سے آستین سمیٹنا۔
تشریح : گردن اتنا پھیرایا کہ سینہ بھی قبلے سے پھر گیا تب تو نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی لیکن اتنا نہیں پھرایا ، بلکہ صرف گردن پھیرا تو یہ مکروہ ہے۔ کتے کی طرح بیٹھنا جسکوا کرو بیٹھنا کہتے ہیں بھی مکروہ ہے، کیونکہ یہ کتے کی طرح شکل بن جاتی ہے۔ سجدے میں دونوں ہاتھوں کو زمین پر بچھا دینا۔ دونوں ہاتھوں سے آستین سمیٹ کر اوپر کو چڑھانا بھی مکروہ ہے ۔ کیونکہ بغیر ضرورت کے کپڑے
Translation: (554) Turning the neck to look, sitting in a squatting posture, spreading both arms in prostration, rolling up both sleeves with both hands. Explanation: If he turns his neck so much that his chest turns from the qibla, then the prayer becomes invalid, but if he turns only the neck, it is makruh. Sitting like a dog (i.e., in the posture of iq'a') is also makruh, because it resembles a dog. Spreading both arms on the ground in prostration, and rolling up sleeves without necessity are also makruh.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This is a ruling statement on three additional disliked actions in salah. The author explains the conditions: turning the neck with the chest still facing qibla is makruh, but if the chest also turns, prayer is invalid. The iq'a' posture (sitting on the heels with knees upright, resembling a dog) is makruh. Spreading the arms on the ground in sujud (like a dog) is makruh. Rolling up sleeves unnecessarily is also makruh. These rulings encourage humility and avoid resemblance to animals or unnecessary fidgeting.

Key Terms
الاقعاء (al-iq'ā'): Squatting on the heels with knees upright, resembling a dog; considered makruh in salah.
افتراش الذراعين (iftirāsh al-dhirā'ayn): Spreading the forearms on the ground in sujud, which is disliked.
تشمير (tashmīr): Rolling up sleeves during prayer, which is makruh if without necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if someone turns only his neck to look sideways during prayer?
A) The prayer becomes invalid.
B) It is makruh but the prayer remains valid.
C) It is permissible and not disliked.
D) It is haram.

Key Takeaway
Turning the neck alone is makruh; only turning the chest from qibla invalidates the prayer; other postures like iq'a' and arm-spreading are also makruh.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Disliked Actions in Prayer with Proofs
Listing disliked acts in prayer with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify five disliked actions in prayer, state the Hanafi ruling (makruh tanzihi) for each, and cite the proof (Quran or hadith) for each.

Core Idea
Disliked actions in prayer do not invalidate it but reduce its reward; the proof texts show they are censured.

The prayer is an intimate conversation with Allah, requiring humility and focus. The Hanafi school specifies certain acts that, while not nullifying the prayer, detract from its perfection. This lesson examines these disliked actions (makruh tanzihi) as derived from Quranic verses and prophetic hadiths, enabling you to refine your prayer practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disliked action
Read through the passage and list each action described as disliked (makruh) in prayer. Note the specific context (e.g., clothing, gesture, sitting posture, grooming).
The passage covers: praying in only trousers while able to wear a shirt, responding to salam by gesture, sitting cross-legged without excuse, tying hair, i'tijar, tashmir, and sadl.
2
Extract the ruling
For each action, confirm the Hanafi ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful). Note that these actions do not invalidate the prayer (as opposed to speaking, which invalidates).
The author uses 'makruh' to mean makruh tanzihi.
3
Trace the dalil
Locate the Quranic verse or hadith cited for each action. Understand the source and its authenticity (most are from Bukhari, Abu Dawud, etc.).
Key proofs include Quran 7:31, hadiths on clothing (Abu Dawud 626), gesture (Muslim 970/431), sitting (Bukhari 828), and general principle of khushu'.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the proof indicates dislike, not prohibition. For example, the command 'take your adornment' implies recommendation, and the Prophet's censure of gestures shows it is not absolute prohibition.
The imperative (amr) in the Quran carries wujub in Hanafi usul, but here the context and other hadiths downgrade it to istihbab or dislike for this particular application.
5
Apply the principle
Consider exceptions: necessity (e.g., no extra cloth, illness) removes the dislike. Understand that these rulings aim to preserve khushu' and dignity in prayer.
The text explicitly mentions that if one has no cloth, prayer in trousers is valid and not disliked.

Source Text
